Franklin">Gene Franklin</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; background: transparent; text-align: left; padding-left: 5px; padding-top: 3px"><a href="/wiki/Astronaut" title="Astronaut">Astronauts</a></div><div class="NavContent hlist" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">The Mercury Seven</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Apollo_astronauts" title="List of Apollo astronauts">Gemini/Apollo astronauts</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Corps" title="NASA Astronaut Corps">NASA Astronaut Corps</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:U.S._space_program_sidebar" title="Template:U.S. space program sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:U.S._space_program_sidebar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Template talk:U.S. space program sidebar (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:U.S._space_program_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Project Mercury</b> was the first <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">human spaceflight</a> program of the United States, running from 1958 through 1963.  An early highlight of the <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a>, its goal was to put a man into Earth <a href="/wiki/Orbital_spaceflight" title="Orbital spaceflight">orbit</a> and return him safely, ideally before the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.  Taken over from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Air_Force" title="US Air Force">US Air Force</a> by the newly created civilian space agency <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, it conducted twenty unmanned developmental flights (some using animals), and six successful flights by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronauts" title="Astronauts">astronauts</a>. The program, which took its name from <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(mythology)" title="Mercury (mythology)">Roman mythology</a>, cost $2.2&#160;billion adjusted for <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lafleur20100308_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-lafleur20100308-2">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[n 2]</a></sup> The astronauts were collectively known as the "<a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">Mercury Seven</a>", and each spacecraft was given a name ending with a "7" by its pilot.\n</p><p>The Space Race began with the 1957 launch of the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">satellite</a> <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a>.  This came as a shock to the American public, and led to the creation of NASA to expedite existing US space exploration efforts, and place most of them under civilian control. After the successful launch of the <a href="/wiki/Explorer_1" title="Explorer 1">Explorer 1</a> satellite in 1958, manned spaceflight became the next goal. The Soviet Union put the first human, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmonaut" title="Cosmonaut">cosmonaut</a> <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a>, into a single orbit aboard <a href="/wiki/Vostok_1" title="Vostok 1">Vostok 1</a> on April 12, 1961. Shortly after this,  on May 5, the US launched its first astronaut, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Alan Shepard</a>, on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suborbital" title="Suborbital">suborbital</a> flight. Soviet <a href="/wiki/Gherman_Titov" title="Gherman Titov">Gherman Titov</a> followed with a day-long orbital flight in August 1961.  The US reached its orbital goal on February 20, 1962, when <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a> made three orbits around the Earth. When Mercury ended in May 1963, both nations had sent six people into space, but the Soviets led the US in total time spent in space.\n</p><p>The Mercury <a href="/wiki/Space_capsule" title="Space capsule">space capsule</a> was produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDonnell_Aircraft" title="McDonnell Aircraft">McDonnell Aircraft</a>, and carried supplies of water, food and oxygen for about one day in a <a href="/wiki/Cabin_pressurization" title="Cabin pressurization">pressurized cabin</a>. Mercury flights were launched from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a> in Florida, on <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicles</a> modified from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Redstone_MRLV" title="Redstone MRLV">Redstone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlas_LV-3B" title="Atlas LV-3B">Atlas D</a> missiles.  The capsule was fitted with a <a href="/wiki/Launch_escape_system" title="Launch escape system">launch escape rocket</a> to carry it safely away from the launch vehicle in case of a failure. The flight was designed to be controlled from the ground via the <a href="/wiki/Manned_Space_Flight_Network" title="Manned Space Flight Network">Manned Space Flight Network</a>, a system of tracking and communications stations; back-up controls were outfitted on board.  Small <a href="/wiki/Retrorocket" title="Retrorocket">retrorockets</a> were used to bring the spacecraft out of its orbit, after which an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ablative_heat_shield" title="Ablative heat shield">ablative heat shield</a> protected it from the heat of  <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">atmospheric reentry</a>. Finally, a <a href="/wiki/Parachute" title="Parachute">parachute</a> slowed the craft for a <a href="/wiki/Splashdown" title="Splashdown">water landing</a>. Both astronaut and capsule were recovered by helicopters deployed from a US Navy ship.\n</p><p>The Mercury project gained popularity, and its missions were followed by millions on radio and TV around the world.  Its success laid the groundwork for <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">Project Gemini</a>, which carried two astronauts in each capsule and perfected space docking maneuvers essential for manned <a href="/wiki/Moon_landing" title="Moon landing">lunar landings</a> in the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a> announced a few weeks after the first manned Mercury flight.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 facilities</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Spacecraft"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Spacecraft</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li flight</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Gallery</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Selection and training</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Mission_profile"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Mission profile</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Suborbital"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Suborbital</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Orbital"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Orbital</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Ground_control"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Ground control</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Flights"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Flights</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Manned"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Manned</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Unmanned"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Unmanned</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Canceled"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Canceled</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Displays"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Displays</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Patches"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Patches</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Videos"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Videos</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Space_program_comparison"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Space program comparison</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Creation">Creation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Creation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Project Mercury was officially approved on October 7, 1958 and publicly announced on December 17.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimwood196312_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimwood196312-7">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966132_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966132-8">[6]</a></sup> Originally called Project Astronaut, President <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" title="Dwight Eisenhower">Dwight Eisenhower</a> felt that gave too much attention to the pilot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192-9">[7]</a></sup> Instead, the name <i><a href="/wiki/Mercury_(mythology)" title="Mercury (mythology)">Mercury</a></i> was chosen from <a href="/wiki/Classical_mythology" title="Classical mythology">classical mythology</a>, which had already lent names to rockets like the Greek <i><a href="/wiki/Atlas_(mythology)" title="Atlas (mythology)">Atlas</a></i> and Roman <i><a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a></i> for the <a href="/wiki/SM-65_Atlas" title="SM-65 Atlas">SM-65</a> and  <a href="/wiki/PGM-19_Jupiter" title="PGM-19 Jupiter">PGM-19</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missiles" title="Missiles">missiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966132_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966132-8">[6]</a></sup> It absorbed military projects with the same aim, such as the Air Force <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man_In_Space_Soonest" title="Man In Space Soonest">Man In Space Soonest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966102_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966102-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[n 3]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Following the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_arms_race" title="Nuclear arms race">nuclear arms race</a> evolved between the US and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> (USSR). Since the USSR did not have bases in the western hemisphere from which to deploy  <a href="/wiki/Bomber" title="Bomber">bomber planes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> decided to develop <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile" title="Intercontinental ballistic missile">intercontinental ballistic missiles</a>, which drove a missile race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200112-14_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200112-14-13">[10]</a></sup> The rocket technology in turn enabled both sides to develop Earth-orbiting satellites for communications, and gathering weather data and <a href="/wiki/Geospatial_intelligence" title="Geospatial intelligence">intelligence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200181_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200181-14">[11]</a></sup> Americans were shocked when the Soviet Union placed the first satellite into orbit in October 1957, leading to a growing fear that the US was falling into a "<a href="/wiki/Missile_gap" title="Missile gap">missile gap</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196628,_52_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196628,_52-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200181_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200181-14">[11]</a></sup>  A month later, the Soviets launched <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_2" title="Sputnik 2">Sputnik 2</a>, carrying a dog into orbit. Though the animal was not recovered alive, it was obvious their goal was manned spaceflight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200155_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200155-16">[13]</a></sup> Unable to disclose details of military space projects, President Eisenhower ordered the creation of a civilian space agency in charge of civilian and scientific space exploration. Based on the  federal research agency <a href="/wiki/National_Advisory_Committee_for_Aeronautics" title="National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics">National Advisory Committee for Aeronautics</a> (NACA), it was named the National Aeronautics and Space Administration (NASA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966113_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966113-17">[14]</a></sup> It achieved its first goal, an American satellite in space, in 1958. The next goal was to put a man there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200157,_82_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200157,_82-18">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The limit of space (also known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karman_line" title="Karman line">Karman line</a>) was defined at the time as a minimum altitude of 62&#160;mi (100&#160;km), and the only way to reach it was by using rocket-powered boosters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200170_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200170-19">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196613_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196613-20">[17]</a></sup> This created risks for the pilot, including explosion, high <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">g-forces</a> and vibrations during lift off through a dense atmosphere,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196644_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196644-21">[18]</a></sup> and temperatures of more than 10,000&#160;&#176;F (5,500&#160;&#176;C) from air compression during reentry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196659_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196659-22">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In space, pilots would require  pressurized chambers or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_suits" title="Space suits">space suits</a> to supply fresh air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001466_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001466-23">[20]</a></sup>  While there, they would experience <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">weightlessness</a>, which could potentially cause disorientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966357_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966357-24">[21]</a></sup> Further potential risks included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_radiation" title="Space radiation">radiation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">micrometeoroid</a> strikes, both of which would normally be absorbed in the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196635,_39&#8211;40_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196635,_39&#8211;40-25">[22]</a></sup>  All seemed possible to overcome: experience from satellites suggested micrometeoroid risk was negligible,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196649_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196649-26">[23]</a></sup> and experiments in the early 1950s with simulated weightlessness, high g-forces on humans, and sending animals to the limit of space, all suggested potential problems could be overcome by known technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196637-38_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196637-38-27">[24]</a></sup> Finally, reentry was studied using the nuclear warheads of ballistic missiles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196661_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196661-28">[25]</a></sup> which demonstrated a blunt, forward-facing heat shield could solve the problem of heating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196661_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196661-28">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organization">Organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/T._Keith_Glennan" title="T. Keith Glennan">T. Keith Glennan</a> had been appointed the first Administrator of NASA, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hugh_L._Dryden" title="Hugh L. Dryden">Hugh L. Dryden</a> (last Director of NACA) as his Deputy, at the creation of the agency on October 1, 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196698&#8211;99_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196698&#8211;99-29">[26]</a></sup> Glennan would report to the president through the <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Council" title="National Space Council">National Aeronautics and Space Council</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200182_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200182-30">[27]</a></sup> The group responsible for Project Mercury was NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Space_Task_Group" title="Space Task Group">Space Task Group</a>, and the goals of the program were to orbit a manned spacecraft around Earth, investigate the pilot\'s ability to function in space, and to recover both pilot and spacecraft safely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966xiii,_134_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966xiii,_134-31">[28]</a></sup> Existing technology and off-the-shelf equipment would be used wherever practical, the simplest and most reliable approach to system design would be followed, and an existing launch vehicle would be employed, together with a progressive test program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966134_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966134-32">[29]</a></sup> Spacecraft requirements included: a <a href="/wiki/Launch_escape_system" title="Launch escape system">launch escape system</a> to separate the spacecraft and its occupant from the <a href="/wiki/Launch_vehicle" title="Launch vehicle">launch vehicle</a> in case of impending failure; <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a> for orientation of the spacecraft in orbit; a <a href="/wiki/Retrorocket" title="Retrorocket">retrorocket</a> system to bring the spacecraft out of orbit; drag braking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blunt_Body_theory" title="Blunt Body theory">blunt body</a> for <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">atmospheric reentry</a>; and landing on water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966134_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966134-32">[29]</a></sup> To communicate with the spacecraft during an orbital mission, an extensive communications network had to be built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966143_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966143-33">[30]</a></sup> In keeping with his desire to keep from giving the US space program an overly military flavor, President Eisenhower at first hesitated to give the project top national priority (DX rating under the <a href="/wiki/Defense_Production_Act" title="Defense Production Act">Defense Production Act</a>), which meant that Mercury had to wait in line behind military projects for materials; however, this rating was granted in May 1959, a little more than a year and a half after Sputnik was launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001157_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001157-34">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contractors_and_facilities">Contractors and facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Contractors and facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Twelve companies bid to build the Mercury spacecraft on a $20 million ($172&#160;million adjusted for inflation) contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966121,_191_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966121,_191-35">[32]</a></sup> In January 1959, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDonnell_Aircraft" title="McDonnell Aircraft">McDonnell Aircraft Corporation</a> was chosen to be prime contractor for the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966137_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966137-36">[33]</a></sup> Two weeks earlier, <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a>, based in Los Angeles, was awarded a contract for <a href="/wiki/Little_Joe_(rocket)" title="Little Joe (rocket)">Little Joe</a>, a small rocket to be used for development of the launch escape system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966124_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966124-37">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[n 4]</a></sup> The World Wide Tracking Network for communication between the ground and spacecraft during a flight was awarded to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Electric" title="Western Electric">Western Electric Company</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966216_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966216-39">[35]</a></sup> Redstone rockets for suborbital launches were manufactured in <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville</a>, Alabama by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrysler_Corporation" title="Chrysler Corporation">Chrysler Corporation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196621_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196621-40">[36]</a></sup> and Atlas rockets by <a href="/wiki/Convair" title="Convair">Convair</a> in San Diego, California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001158_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001158-41">[37]</a></sup> For manned launches, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic_Missile_Range" title="Atlantic Missile Range">Atlantic Missile Range</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">Cape Canaveral Air Force Station</a> in Florida was made available by the USAF.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200189&#8211;90_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200189&#8211;90-42">[38]</a></sup> This was also the site of the Mercury Control Center while the computing center of the communication network was in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goddard_Space_Center" title="Goddard Space Center">Goddard Space Center</a>, Maryland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200186_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200186-43">[39]</a></sup> Little Joe rockets were launched from <a href="/wiki/Wallops_Island" title="Wallops Island">Wallops Island</a>, Virginia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966141_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966141-44">[40]</a></sup> Astronaut training took place at <a href="/wiki/Langley_Research_Center" title="Langley Research Center">Langley Research Center</a> in Virginia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_Flight_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Lewis Flight Propulsion Laboratory">Lewis Flight Propulsion Laboratory</a> in Cleveland, Ohio, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Development_Center" title="Naval Air Development Center">Naval Air Development Center Johnsville</a> in Warminster, PA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001103-110_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001103-110-45">[41]</a></sup> Langley wind tunnels<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196688_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196688-46">[42]</a></sup> together with a rocket sled track at <a href="/wiki/Holloman_Air_Force_Base" title="Holloman Air Force Base">Holloman Air Force Base</a> at Alamogordo, New Mexico were used for aerodynamic studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001248_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001248-47">[43]</a></sup> Both Navy and Air Force aircraft were made available for the development of the spacecraft\'s landing system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001172-173_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001172-173-48">[44]</a></sup> and Navy ships and Navy and Marine Corps helicopters were made available for recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[n 5]</a></sup> South of Cape Canaveral the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cocoa_Beach" title="Cocoa Beach">Cocoa Beach</a> boomed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CocoaBeach_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-CocoaBeach-51">[46]</a></sup> From here, 75,000 people watched the first American orbital flight being launched in 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CocoaBeach_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-CocoaBeach-51">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 275px"><div style="width: 275px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 270px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wallops_Island_-_GPN-2000-001888.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2205" data-file-width="2946" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Wallops_Island_-_GPN-2000-001888.jpg/240px-Wallops_Island_-_GPN-2000-001888.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Wallops_Island_-_GPN-2000-001888.jpg/360px-Wallops_Island_-_GPN-2000-001888.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Wallops_Island_-_GPN-2000-001888.jpg/480px-Wallops_Island_-_GPN-2000-001888.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Wallops_Island" title="Wallops Island">Wallops Island</a> test facility, 1961\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0.15em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 275px"><div style="width: 275px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 270px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_control_center_4june1963.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2090" data-file-width="2785" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mercury_control_center_4june1963.jpg/240px-Mercury_control_center_4june1963.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mercury_control_center_4june1963.jpg/360px-Mercury_control_center_4june1963.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mercury_control_center_4june1963.jpg/480px-Mercury_control_center_4june1963.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Mercury_Control_Center" title="Mercury Control Center">Mercury Control Center</a>, Cape Canaveral, 1963\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 360px"><div style="width: 360px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 355px;"><div style="margin:15.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Project-Mercury-facility-map.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1018" data-file-width="1844" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Project-Mercury-facility-map.png/325px-Project-Mercury-facility-map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Project-Mercury-facility-map.png/488px-Project-Mercury-facility-map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Project-Mercury-facility-map.png/650px-Project-Mercury-facility-map.png 2x" width="325"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Location of production and operational facilities of Project Mercury\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft">Spacecraft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Spacecraft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Mercury spacecraft\'s principal designer was <a href="/wiki/Maxime_Faget" title="Maxime Faget">Maxime Faget</a>, who started research for manned spaceflight during the time of the NACA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001150_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001150-52">[47]</a></sup> It was 10.8 feet (3.3&#160;m) long and 6.0 feet (1.8&#160;m) wide; with the launch escape system added, the overall length was 25.9 feet (7.9&#160;m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001131_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001131-53">[48]</a></sup> With 100 cubic feet (2.8&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of habitable volume, the capsule was just large enough for a single crew member.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196647_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196647-54">[49]</a></sup> Inside were 120 controls: 55 electrical switches, 30 <a href="/wiki/Fuse_(electrical)" title="Fuse (electrical)">fuses</a> and 35 mechanical levers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966245_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966245-55">[50]</a></sup> The heaviest spacecraft, Mercury-Atlas 9, weighed 3,000 pounds (1,400&#160;kg) fully loaded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966490_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966490-56">[51]</a></sup> Its outer skin was made of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_41" title="Ren&#233; 41">Ren&#233; 41</a>, a nickel alloy able to withstand high temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001136_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001136-57">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The spacecraft was cone shaped, with a neck at the narrow end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001131_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001131-53">[48]</a></sup> It had a convex base, which carried a heat shield (Item <b>2</b> in the diagram below)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001134&#8211;136_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001134&#8211;136-58">[53]</a></sup> consisting of an aluminum <a href="/wiki/Honeycomb_structure" title="Honeycomb structure">honeycomb</a> covered with multiple layers of <a href="/wiki/Fiberglass" title="Fiberglass">fiberglass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966140,_143_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966140,_143-59">[54]</a></sup> Strapped to it was a retropack (<b>1</b>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132&#8211;134_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132&#8211;134-60">[55]</a></sup> consisting of three rockets deployed to brake the spacecraft during reentry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132-61">[56]</a></sup> Between these were three minor rockets for separating the spacecraft from the launch vehicle at orbital insertion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966188_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966188-62">[57]</a></sup> The straps that held the package could be severed when it was no longer needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001134_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001134-63">[58]</a></sup> Next to the heat shield was the pressurized crew compartment (<b>3</b>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001136&#8211;144_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001136&#8211;144-64">[59]</a></sup> Inside, an astronaut would be strapped to a form-fitting seat with instruments in front of him and with his back to the heat shield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001136-137_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001136-137-65">[60]</a></sup> Underneath the seat was the environmental control system supplying oxygen and heat,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001138_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001138-66">[61]</a></sup> scrubbing the air of CO<sub>2</sub>, vapor and odors, and (on orbital flights) collecting urine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001139_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001139-67">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[n 6]</a></sup> The recovery compartment (<b>4</b>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144&#8211;145_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144&#8211;145-70">[64]</a></sup> at the narrow end of the spacecraft contained three parachutes: a drogue to stabilize free fall and two main chutes, a primary and reserve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144-71">[65]</a></sup> Between the heat shield and inner wall of the crew compartment was a landing skirt, deployed by letting down the heat shield before landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001135_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001135-72">[66]</a></sup> On top of the recovery compartment was the <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(radio)" title="Antenna (radio)">antenna</a> section (<b>5</b>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001145&#8211;148_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001145&#8211;148-73">[67]</a></sup> containing both antennas for communication and scanners for guiding spacecraft orientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001147_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001147-74">[68]</a></sup> Attached was a flap used to ensure  the spacecraft was faced heat shield first during reentry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966199_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966199-75">[69]</a></sup> A launch escape system (<b>6</b>) was mounted to the narrow end of the spacecraft<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001179&#8211;181_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001179&#8211;181-76">[70]</a></sup> containing three small solid-fueled rockets which could be fired briefly in a launch failure to separate the capsule safely from its booster. It would deploy the capsule\'s parachute for a landing nearby at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001179_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001179-77">[71]</a></sup> (See also <a href="#Mission_profile">Mission profile</a> for details.)\n</p><p>The Mercury spacecraft did not have an on-board computer, instead relying on all computation for reentry to be calculated by computers on the ground, with their results (retrofire times and firing attitude) then transmitted to the spacecraft by radio while in flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAComp1_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASAComp1-78">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DanCompSpace_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-DanCompSpace-79">[73]</a></sup> All computer systems used in the Mercury space program were housed in <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> facilities on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASAComp1_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASAComp1-78">[72]</a></sup> The computer systems were <a href="/wiki/IBM_701" title="IBM 701">IBM 701</a> computers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBMArchSpace_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-IBMArchSpace-80">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IBM701_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-IBM701-81">[75]</a></sup> (See also <a href="#Ground_control">Ground control</a> for details.)\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 425px"><div style="width: 425px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 420px;"><div style="margin:20.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury-spacecraft-color.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="212" data-file-width="520" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Mercury-spacecraft-color.png/390px-Mercury-spacecraft-color.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/Mercury-spacecraft-color.png 1.5x" width="390"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>1. Retropack. 2. Heatshield. 3. Crew compartment. 4. Recovery compartment. 5. Antenna section. 6. Launch escape system.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0.15em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:McDonnellMercuryCapsule1.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2318" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/McDonnellMercuryCapsule1.jpg/169px-McDonnellMercuryCapsule1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/McDonnellMercuryCapsule1.jpg/253px-McDonnellMercuryCapsule1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/McDonnellMercuryCapsule1.jpg/338px-McDonnellMercuryCapsule1.jpg 2x" width="169"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Retropack: Retrorockets with red posigrade rockets\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 310px"><div style="width: 310px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 305px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Landing-skirt.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="455" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Landing-skirt.jpg/275px-Landing-skirt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Landing-skirt.jpg/413px-Landing-skirt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/16/Landing-skirt.jpg 2x" width="275"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Landing skirt (or bag) deployment: skirt is inflated; on impact the air is pressed out (like an <a href="/wiki/Airbag" title="Airbag">airbag</a>)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilot_accommodations">Pilot accommodations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Pilot accommodations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GPN-2000-001027.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2343" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/GPN-2000-001027.jpg/170px-GPN-2000-001027.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/GPN-2000-001027.jpg/255px-GPN-2000-001027.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/GPN-2000-001027.jpg/340px-GPN-2000-001027.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GPN-2000-001027.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">John Glenn</a> wearing his Mercury space suit</div></div></div>\n<p>The astronaut lay in a sitting position with his back to the heat shield, which was found to be the position that best enabled a human to withstand the high <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">g-forces</a> of launch and reentry. A fiberglass seat was custom-molded from each astronaut\'s space-suited body for maximum support. Near his left hand was a manual abort handle to activate the launch escape system if necessary  prior to or during liftoff, in case the automatic trigger failed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001142_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001142-82">[76]</a></sup>\n</p><p>To supplement the onboard environmental control system, he wore a <a href="/wiki/Pressure_suit" title="Pressure suit">pressure suit</a> with its own <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> supply, which would also cool him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001191_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001191-83">[77]</a></sup> A cabin atmosphere of pure oxygen at a low pressure of 5.5 psi (equivalent to an altitude of 24,800 feet (7,600&#160;m)) was chosen, rather than one with the same composition as air (<a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>/oxygen) at sea level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGatland1976264_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGatland1976264-84">[78]</a></sup> This was easier to control,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001410_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001410-85">[79]</a></sup> avoided the risk of <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a> ("the bends"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiblin1998_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiblin1998-86">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[n 7]</a></sup> and also saved on spacecraft weight. Fires (which never occurred) would have to be extinguished by emptying the cabin of oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001139_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001139-67">[62]</a></sup> In such case, or failure of the cabin pressure for any reason, the astronaut could make an emergency return to Earth, relying on his suit for survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196648&#8211;49_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196648&#8211;49-88">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001139_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001139-67">[62]</a></sup> The astronauts normally flew with their <a href="/wiki/Visor" title="Visor">visor</a> up, which meant that the suit was not inflated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001139_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001139-67">[62]</a></sup> With the visor down and the suit inflated, the astronaut could only reach the side and bottom panels, where vital buttons and handles were placed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966246_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966246-89">[82]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The astronaut also wore <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrodes" title="Electrodes">electrodes</a> on his chest to record his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_rhythm" title="Heart rhythm">heart rhythm</a>, a cuff that could take his blood pressure, and a <a href="/wiki/Medical_thermometer" title="Medical thermometer">rectal thermometer</a> to record his temperature (this was replaced by an oral thermometer on the last flight).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001191,_194_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001191,_194-90">[83]</a></sup> Data from these was sent to the ground during the flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001191_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001191-83">[77]</a></sup> The astronaut normally drank water and ate food pellets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001343-344_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001343-344-91">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[n 8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Once in orbit, the spacecraft could be rotated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaw,_pitch,_and_roll" title="Yaw, pitch, and roll">yaw, pitch, and roll</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agle199809_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-agle199809-93">[85]</a></sup> Along its longitudinal axis (roll), left to right from the astronaut\'s point of view (yaw), and up or down (pitch).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001142&#8211;143_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001142&#8211;143-94">[86]</a></sup> Movement was created by <a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine" title="Rocket engine">rocket-propelled</a> thrusters which used <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a> as a fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966499_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966499-95">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001143_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001143-96">[88]</a></sup> For orientation, the pilot could look through the window in front of him or he could look at a screen connected to a <a href="/wiki/Periscope" title="Periscope">periscope</a> with a camera which could be turned 360&#176;.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001141_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001141-97">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Mercury astronauts had taken part in the development of their spacecraft, and insisted that manual control, and a window, be elements of its design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200198&#8211;99_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200198&#8211;99-98">[90]</a></sup> As a result, spacecraft movement and other functions could be controlled three ways: remotely from the ground when passing over a ground station, automatically guided by onboard instruments, or manually by the astronaut, who could replace or override the two other methods. Experience validated the astronauts\' insistence on manual controls.  Without them, Gordon Cooper\'s manual reentry during the last flight would not have been possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966501_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966501-99">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;border:solid 1px #aaa;background:#F9F9F9;padding-left:10px;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:left;;"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Cutaways and interior of spacecraft</div></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">\n<dl><dt>Spacecraft cutaway</dt></dl>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li spacecraft\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 335px"><div style="width: 335px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 330px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" of rotation for the spacecraft: yaw, pitch and roll\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 335px"><div style="width: 335px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 330px;"><div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Temperature profile for spacecraft in <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">Fahrenheit</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<dl><dt>Control panels and handle</dt></dl>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0.15em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 485px"><div style="width: 485px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 480px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Control_panels_mercury_atlas_6.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="865" data-file-width="1299" decoding="async" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Control_panels_mercury_atlas_6.png/900px-Control_panels_mercury_atlas_6.png 2x" width="450"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The control panels of <i>Friendship 7</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnknown19628_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnknown19628-100">[92]</a></sup> The panels changed between flights, among others the periscope screen that dominates the center of these panels was dropped for the final flight together with the periscope itself.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 305px"><div style="width: 305px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 300px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three-axis_hand_controller_mercury_project.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="915" data-file-width="816" decoding="async" height="300" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Three-axis_hand_controller_mercury_project.jpg/401px-Three-axis_hand_controller_mercury_project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Three-axis_hand_controller_mercury_project.jpg/535px-Three-axis_hand_controller_mercury_project.jpg 2x" width="267"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>3-axis handle for attitude control\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n</div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_and_production">Development and production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Development and production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Technicians_working_in_the_McDonnell_White_Room_on_the_Mercury-crop.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2487" data-file-width="1901" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Technicians_working_in_the_McDonnell_White_Room_on_the_Mercury-crop.jpg/170px-Technicians_working_in_the_McDonnell_White_Room_on_the_Mercury-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Technicians_working_in_the_McDonnell_White_Room_on_the_Mercury-crop.jpg/255px-Technicians_working_in_the_McDonnell_White_Room_on_the_Mercury-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Technicians_working_in_the_McDonnell_White_Room_on_the_Mercury-crop.jpg/340px-Technicians_working_in_the_McDonnell_White_Room_on_the_Mercury-crop.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Technicians_working_in_the_McDonnell_White_Room_on_the_Mercury-crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spacecraft production in clean room at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDonnell_Aircraft" title="McDonnell Aircraft">McDonnell Aircraft</a>, St. Louis, 1960</div></div></div>\n<p>The Mercury spacecraft design was modified three times by NASA between 1958 and 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001152_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001152-101">[93]</a></sup> After bidding by potential contractors had been completed, NASA selected the design submitted as "C" in November 1958.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001153_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001153-102">[94]</a></sup> After it failed a test flight in July 1959, a final configuration, "D", emerged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001159_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001159-103">[95]</a></sup> The heat shield shape had been developed earlier in the 1950s through experiments with ballistic missiles, which had shown a blunt profile would create a shock wave that would lead most of the heat around the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001149_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001149-104">[96]</a></sup> To further protect against heat, either a <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sink</a>, or an ablative material, could be added to the shield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196663_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196663-105">[97]</a></sup> The heat sink would remove heat by the flow of the air inside the shock wave, whereas the ablative heat shield would remove heat by a controlled evaporation of the ablative material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196664_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196664-106">[98]</a></sup> After unmanned tests, the latter was chosen for manned flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966206_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966206-107">[99]</a></sup> Apart from the capsule design, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_plane" title="Rocket plane">rocket plane</a> similar to the existing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-15" title="X-15">X-15</a> was considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196678-80_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196678-80-108">[100]</a></sup> This approach was still too far from being able to make a spaceflight, and was consequently dropped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196672_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196672-109">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[n 9]</a></sup> The heat shield and the stability of the spacecraft were tested in <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_wind_tunnel" title="Supersonic wind tunnel">wind tunnels</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196688_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196688-46">[42]</a></sup> and later in flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001229_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001229-114">[105]</a></sup> The launch escape system was developed through unmanned flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001196_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001196-115">[106]</a></sup> During a period of problems with development of the landing parachutes, alternative landing systems such as the <a href="/wiki/Rogallo_wing" title="Rogallo wing">Rogallo glider wing</a> were considered, but ultimately scrapped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966198_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966198-116">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The spacecraft were produced at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/McDonnell_Aircraft" title="McDonnell Aircraft">McDonnell Aircraft</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis</a>, Missouri in clean rooms and tested in vacuum chambers at the McDonnell plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132,_159_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132,_159-117">[108]</a></sup> The spacecraft had close to 600 subcontractors, such as <a href="/wiki/Garrett_AiResearch" title="Garrett AiResearch">Garrett AiResearch</a> which built the spacecraft\'s environmental control system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966137_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966137-36">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001138_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001138-66">[61]</a></sup> Final quality control and preparations of the spacecraft were made at Hangar S at Cape Canaveral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001184-188_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001184-188-118">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[n 10]</a></sup> NASA ordered 20 production spacecraft, numbered 1 through 20.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966137_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966137-36">[33]</a></sup> Five of the 20, Nos. 10, 12, 15, 17, and 19, were not flown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963235&#8211;238_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963235&#8211;238-122">[112]</a></sup> Spacecraft No. 3 and No. 4 were destroyed during unmanned test flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963235&#8211;238_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963235&#8211;238-122">[112]</a></sup> Spacecraft No. 11 sank and was recovered from the bottom of the Atlantic Ocean after 38 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963235&#8211;238_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963235&#8211;238-122">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001402&#8211;405_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001402&#8211;405-123">[113]</a></sup> Some spacecraft were modified after initial production (refurbished after launch abort, modified for longer missions, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[n 11]</a></sup> A number of Mercury <a href="/wiki/Boilerplate_(spaceflight)" title="Boilerplate (spaceflight)">boilerplate spacecraft</a> (made from non-flight materials or lacking production spacecraft systems) were also made by NASA and McDonnell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966126_&amp;_138_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966126_&amp;_138-127">[116]</a></sup> They were designed and used to test spacecraft recovery systems and the escape tower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196696,_105_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196696,_105-128">[117]</a></sup> McDonnell also built the spacecraft simulators used by the astronauts during training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001107_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001107-129">[118]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 245px"><div style="width: 245px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 240px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heatshield-test3.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="847" data-file-width="1033" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Heatshield-test3.jpg/207px-Heatshield-test3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Heatshield-test3.jpg/311px-Heatshield-test3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Heatshield-test3.jpg/415px-Heatshield-test3.jpg 2x" width="207"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Shadowgraph" title="Shadowgraph">Shadowgraph</a> of the reentry <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> simulated in a <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_wind_tunnel" title="Supersonic wind tunnel">wind tunnel</a>, 1957\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0.15em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 1958&#8211;59\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 265px"><div style="width: 265px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 260px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_Space_Capsule-wind-tunnel.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1925" data-file-width="2570" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Mercury_Space_Capsule-wind-tunnel.jpg/227px-Mercury_Space_Capsule-wind-tunnel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Mercury_Space_Capsule-wind-tunnel.jpg/341px-Mercury_Space_Capsule-wind-tunnel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Mercury_Space_Capsule-wind-tunnel.jpg/454px-Mercury_Space_Capsule-wind-tunnel.jpg 2x" width="227"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Experiment with boilerplate spacecraft, 1959\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;border:solid 1px #aaa;background:#F9F9F9;padding-left:10px;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:left;;"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Development of earth landing system</div></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 735px"><div style="width: 735px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 730px;"><div style="margin:17px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury-project-earth-landing-system-test.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1139" data-file-width="1488" decoding="async" height="536" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Mercury-project-earth-landing-system-test.png/700px-Mercury-project-earth-landing-system-test.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Mercury-project-earth-landing-system-test.png/1050px-Mercury-project-earth-landing-system-test.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Mercury-project-earth-landing-system-test.png/1400px-Mercury-project-earth-landing-system-test.png 2x" width="700"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Drop of boilerplate spacecraft in training of landing and recovery. 56 such qualification tests were made together with tests of individual steps of the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001172-173_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001172-173-48">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n</div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_vehicles">Launch vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Launch vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury-launch-vehicles.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1534" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Mercury-launch-vehicles.png/310px-Mercury-launch-vehicles.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Mercury-launch-vehicles.png/465px-Mercury-launch-vehicles.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Mercury-launch-vehicles.png/620px-Mercury-launch-vehicles.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury-launch-vehicles.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Launch vehicles: 1. Mercury-Atlas (orbital flights). 2. Mercury-Redstone (suborbital flights). 3. Little Joe (unmanned tests)</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_escape_system_testing">Launch escape system testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Launch escape system testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A small launch vehicle (55 feet (17&#160;m) long) called <a href="/wiki/Little_Joe_(rocket)" title="Little Joe (rocket)">Little Joe</a> was used for unmanned tests of the launch escape system, using a Mercury capsule with an escape tower mounted on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001197_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001197-130">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966638_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966638-131">[120]</a></sup> Its main purpose was to test the system at a point called max-q, at which air pressure against the spacecraft peaked, making separation of the launch vehicle and spacecraft most difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001223_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001223-132">[121]</a></sup> It was also the point at which the astronaut was subjected to the heaviest vibrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001284_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001284-133">[122]</a></sup> The Little Joe rocket used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid-fuel_rocket" title="Solid-fuel rocket">solid-fuel</a> propellant and was originally designed in 1958 by the NACA for suborbital manned flights, but was redesigned for Project Mercury to simulate an Atlas-D launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001196_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001196-115">[106]</a></sup> It was produced by <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001197_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001197-130">[119]</a></sup> It was not able to change direction; instead its flight depended on the angle from which it was launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001198_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001198-134">[123]</a></sup> Its maximum altitude was 100&#160;mi (160&#160;km) fully loaded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966125_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966125-135">[124]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Scout_1" title="Mercury-Scout 1">Scout launch vehicle</a> was used for a single flight intended to evaluate the tracking network; however, it failed and was destroyed from the ground shortly after launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966392&#8211;397_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966392&#8211;397-136">[125]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suborbital_flight">Suborbital flight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Suborbital flight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Redstone_Launch_Vehicle" title="Mercury-Redstone Launch Vehicle">Mercury-Redstone Launch Vehicle</a>, an 83-foot (25&#160;m) tall (with capsule and escape system) single-stage launch vehicle used for suborbital (<a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile" title="Ballistic missile">ballistic</a>) flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001206_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001206-137">[126]</a></sup> It had a liquid-fueled engine that burned alcohol and liquid oxygen producing about 75,000 pounds of thrust, which was not enough for orbital missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001206_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001206-137">[126]</a></sup> It was a descendant of the German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/V-2_(rocket)" title="V-2 (rocket)">V-2</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196621_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196621-40">[36]</a></sup> and developed for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army" title="U.S. Army">U.S. Army</a> during the early 1950s. It was modified for Project Mercury by removing the warhead and adding a collar for supporting the spacecraft together with material for damping vibrations during launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001207_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001207-138">[127]</a></sup> Its rocket motor was produced by <a href="/wiki/North_American_Aviation" title="North American Aviation">North American Aviation</a> and its direction could be altered during flight by its fins. They worked in two ways: by directing the air around them, or by directing the thrust by their inner parts (or both at the same time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196621_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196621-40">[36]</a></sup> Both the Atlas-D and Redstone launch vehicles contained an automatic abort sensing system which allowed them to abort a launch by firing the launch escape system if something went wrong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001209,_214_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001209,_214-139">[128]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mercury-Jupiter" title="Mercury-Jupiter">Jupiter</a> rocket, also developed by Von Braun\'s team at the Redstone Arsenal in Huntsville, was considered as well for intermediate Mercury suborbital flights at a higher speed and altitude than Redstone, but this plan was dropped when it turned out that man-rating Jupiter for the Mercury program would actually cost more than flying an Atlas due to economics of scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966151_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966151-140">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimwood196369_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimwood196369-141">[130]</a></sup> Jupiter\'s only use other than as a missile system was for the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Juno_II" title="Juno II">Juno II</a> launch vehicle and keeping a full staff of technical personnel around solely to fly a few Mercury capsules would result in excessively high costs.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_flight">Orbital flight</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Orbital flight">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Orbital missions required use of the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_LV-3B" title="Atlas LV-3B">Atlas LV-3B</a>, a man-rated version of the <a href="/wiki/SM-65D_Atlas" title="SM-65D Atlas">Atlas D</a> which was originally developed as the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>\' first operational <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile" title="Intercontinental ballistic missile">intercontinental ballistic missile (ICBM)</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001211_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001211-142">[131]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Convair" title="Convair">Convair</a> for the Air Force during the mid-1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196622_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196622-143">[132]</a></sup> The Atlas was a "one-and-one-half-stage" rocket fueled by <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a> and liquid <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (LOX).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001211_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001211-142">[131]</a></sup> The rocket by itself stood 67 feet (20&#160;m) high; total height of the Atlas-Mercury space vehicle at launch was 95 feet (29&#160;m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001212_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001212-144">[133]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Atlas first stage was a booster skirt with two engines burning liquid fuel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001225,_250_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001225,_250-145">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[n 12]</a></sup> This together with the larger sustainer second stage gave it sufficient power to launch a Mercury spacecraft into orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001211_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001211-142">[131]</a></sup> Both stages fired from lift-off with the thrust from the second stage sustainer engine passing through an opening in the first stage. After separation from the first stage, the sustainer stage continued alone. The sustainer also steered the rocket by thrusters guided by gyroscopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001458-459_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001458-459-147">[135]</a></sup> Smaller vernier rockets were added on its sides for precise control of maneuvers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001211_142-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001211-142">[131]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span><span
