mounted - on launch pad at Launch Complex 14\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronauts">Astronauts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Astronauts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Project_Mercury-Mercury_Seven-Astronauts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2105" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Project_Mercury-Mercury_Seven-Astronauts.jpg/300px-Project_Mercury-Mercury_Seven-Astronauts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Project_Mercury-Mercury_Seven-Astronauts.jpg/450px-Project_Mercury-Mercury_Seven-Astronauts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Project_Mercury-Mercury_Seven-Astronauts.jpg/600px-Project_Mercury-Mercury_Seven-Astronauts.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Project_Mercury-Mercury_Seven-Astronauts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Left to right: <a href="/wiki/Gus_Grissom" title="Gus Grissom">Grissom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Shepard" title="Alan Shepard">Shepard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scott_Carpenter" title="Scott Carpenter">Carpenter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wally_Schirra" title="Wally Schirra">Schirra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deke_Slayton" title="Deke Slayton">Slayton</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Glenn" title="John Glenn">Glenn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Cooper" title="Gordon Cooper">Cooper</a>, 1962</div></div></div>\n<p>NASA announced the following seven astronauts &#8211; known as the <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Seven" title="Mercury Seven">Mercury Seven</a> &#8211; on April 9, 1959:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966164_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966164-148">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640-149">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Name\n</th>\n<th>Rank\n</th>\n<th>Unit\n</th>\n<th>Born\n</th>\n<th>Died\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td data-sort-value="Carpenter, Scott"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M._Scott_Carpenter" title="M. Scott Carpenter">M. Scott Carpenter</a>\n</td>\n<td>Lt (later Cdr)\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">USN</a>\n</td>\n<td>1925\n</td>\n<td>2013\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td data-sort-value="Cooper, Gordon"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L._Gordon_Cooper" title="L. Gordon Cooper">L. Gordon Cooper</a>\n</td>\n<td>Capt (later Col)\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">USAF</a>\n</td>\n<td>1927\n</td>\n<td>2004\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td data-sort-value="Glenn, John"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_H._Glenn,_Jr." title="John H. Glenn, Jr.">John H. Glenn, Jr.</a>\n</td>\n<td>Maj (later Col)\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">USMC</a>\n</td>\n<td>1921\n</td>\n<td>2016\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td data-sort-value="Grissom, Gus"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virgil_I._Grissom" title="Virgil I. Grissom">Virgil I. Grissom</a>\n</td>\n<td>Capt (later Lt Col)\n</td>\n<td>USAF\n</td>\n<td>1926\n</td>\n<td>1967\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td data-sort-value="Schirra, Wally"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walter_M._Schirra,_Jr." title="Walter M. Schirra, Jr.">Walter M. Schirra, Jr.</a>\n</td>\n<td>Lt Cdr (later Capt)\n</td>\n<td>USN\n</td>\n<td>1923\n</td>\n<td>2007\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td data-sort-value="Shepard, Alan"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alan_B._Shepard,_Jr." title="Alan B. Shepard, Jr.">Alan B. Shepard, Jr.</a>\n</td>\n<td>Lt Cdr (later Radm)\n</td>\n<td>USN\n</td>\n<td>1923\n</td>\n<td>1998\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td data-sort-value="Slayton, Deke"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Donald_K._Slayton" title="Donald K. Slayton">Donald K. Slayton</a>\n</td>\n<td>Maj\n</td>\n<td>USAF\n</td>\n<td>1924\n</td>\n<td>1993\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Shepard became the first American in space by making a suborbital flight in May 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966341_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966341-150">[138]</a></sup> He went on to fly in the Apollo program and became the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">only Mercury astronaut to walk on the Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001445_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001445-151">[139]</a></sup> Gus Grissom, who became the second American in space, also participated in the Gemini and Apollo programs, but died in January 1967 during a pre-launch test for <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">Apollo 1</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001442_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001442-152">[140]</a></sup> Glenn became the first American to orbit the Earth in February 1962, then quit NASA and went into politics, serving as a US Senator from 1974 to 1999, and returned to space in 1998 as a Payload Specialist aboard <a href="/wiki/STS-95" title="STS-95">STS-95</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001440,441_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001440,441-153">[141]</a></sup> Deke Slayton was grounded in 1962, but remained with NASA and was appointed Chief Astronaut at the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">Project Gemini</a>. He remained in the position of senior astronaut, in charge of space crew flight assignments among many other responsibilities, until towards the end of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Project_Apollo" title="Project Apollo">Project Apollo</a>, when he resigned and began training to fly on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo-Soyuz_Test_Project" title="Apollo-Soyuz Test Project">Apollo-Soyuz Test Project</a> in 1975, which he successfully did.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001446-447_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001446-447-154">[142]</a></sup> Gordon Cooper became the last to fly in Mercury and made its longest flight, and also flew a Gemini mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640&#8211;641_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640&#8211;641-155">[143]</a></sup> Carpenter\'s Mercury flight was his only trip into space. Schirra flew the third orbital Mercury mission, and then flew a Gemini mission. Three years later, he commanded the first manned Apollo mission, becoming the only person to fly in all three of those programs.\n</p><p>One of the astronauts\' tasks was publicity; they gave interviews to the press and visited project manufacturing facilities to speak with those who worked on Project Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200199_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200199-156">[144]</a></sup> To make their travels easier, they requested and got jet fighters for personal use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001104_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001104-157">[145]</a></sup> The press was especially fond of John Glenn, who was considered the best speaker of the seven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200196_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200196-158">[146]</a></sup> They sold their personal stories to <a href="/wiki/Life_(magazine)" title="Life (magazine)"><i>Life</i></a> magazine which portrayed them as patriotic, God-fearing family men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001100_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001100-159">[147]</a></sup> <i>Life</i> was also allowed to be at home with the families while the astronauts were in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001100_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001100-159">[147]</a></sup> During the project, Grissom, Carpenter, Cooper, Schirra and Slayton stayed with their families at or near Langley Air Force Base; Glenn lived at the base and visited his family in Washington DC on weekends. Shepard lived with his family at <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Oceana" title="Naval Air Station Oceana">Naval Air Station Oceana</a> in Virginia.\n</p><p>Other than Grissom, who was killed in the 1967 <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">Apollo 1</a> fire, the other six survived past retirement<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200197_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200197-160">[148]</a></sup> and died between 1993 and 2016.\n</p>\n<div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;border:solid 1px #aaa;background:#F9F9F9;padding-left:10px;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:left;;"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Astronauts\' assignments</div></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 905px"><div style="width: 905px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 900px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AstronautAssignmentsChart-Mercury7.PNG"><img alt="" data-file-height="662" data-file-width="1436" decoding="async" height="400" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/AstronautAssignmentsChart-Mercury7.PNG/868px-AstronautAssignmentsChart-Mercury7.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/AstronautAssignmentsChart-Mercury7.PNG/1302px-AstronautAssignmentsChart-Mercury7.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/AstronautAssignmentsChart-Mercury7.PNG 2x" width="868"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Mercury 7 astronaut assignments. Schirra had the most flights with three; Glenn, though being the first to leave NASA, had the last with a <a href="/wiki/STS-95" title="STS-95">Space Shuttle mission</a> in 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glenn1998_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glenn1998-161">[149]</a></sup> Shepard was the only one to walk on the Moon.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n</div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Selection_and_training">Selection and training</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Selection and training">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Prior to Project Mercury, there was no protocol for selecting astronauts so NASA would set a far reaching precedent with both their selection process and initial choices for astronaut. At the end of 1958, various ideas for the selection pool were discussed privately within the national government and the civilian space program, and also among the public at large. Initially, there was the idea to issue a widespread public call to volunteers. Thrill seekers such as rock climbers and acrobats would have been allowed to apply but this idea was quickly shot down by NASA officials who understood that an undertaking such as space flight required individuals with professional training and education in flight engineering. By late 1958, NASA officials decided to move forward with test pilots being the heart of their selection pool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-162">[150]</a></sup> On President Eisenhower\'s insistence, the group was further narrowed down to active duty military <a href="/wiki/Test_pilot" title="Test pilot">test pilots</a>, which set the number of candidates at 508 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966160&#8211;161_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966160&#8211;161-163">[151]</a></sup> who were either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USN" title="USN">USN</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USMC" title="USMC">USMC</a> <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Aviator" title="United States Naval Aviator">naval aviation pilots</a> (NAPs), or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USAF" title="USAF">USAF</a> <a href="/wiki/U.S._Air_Force_aeronautical_rating#USAF_rating_requirements" title="U.S. Air Force aeronautical rating">pilots of Senior or Command rating</a>. These men had long military records which would give NASA officials more background information to base their decisions on. Furthermore, these men were adept at flying the most advanced aircraft to date, giving them the best qualifications for the new position of astronaut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-162">[150]</a></sup> However, this selection excluded women since there were no female military test pilots at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192-9">[7]</a></sup> It also excluded civilian NASA <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-15" title="X-15">X-15</a> pilot <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">Neil Armstrong</a>, though he had been selected by the US Air Force in 1958 for its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man_In_Space_Soonest" title="Man In Space Soonest">Man In Space Soonest</a> program, which was replaced by Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005173_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005173-164">[152]</a></sup> Although Armstrong had been a combat-experienced NAP during the Korean War, he left active duty in 1952.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[n 13]</a></sup> Armstrong became NASA\'s first civilian astronaut in 1962 when he was selected for NASA\'s second group,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005201&#8211;202_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005201&#8211;202-167">[154]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">became the first man on the Moon</a> in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson200917_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson200917-168">[155]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It was further stipulated that candidates should be between 25 and 40 years old, no taller than 5&#160;ft 11&#160;in (1.80&#160;m), and hold a college degree in a <a href="/wiki/Science,_technology,_engineering,_and_mathematics" title="Science, technology, engineering, and mathematics">STEM</a> subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192-9">[7]</a></sup> The college degree requirement excluded the USAF\'s <a href="/wiki/Bell_X-1" title="Bell X-1">X-1</a> pilot, then-Lt Col (later Brig Gen) <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Yeager" title="Chuck Yeager">Chuck Yeager</a>, the first person to exceed the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192&#8211;93_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192&#8211;93-169">[156]</a></sup> He later became a critic of the project, ridiculing the civilian space program, labeling astronauts as "spam in a can." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[157]</a></sup> John Glenn did not have a college degree either, but used influential friends to make the selection committee accept him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001440_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001440-171">[158]</a></sup> USAF Capt (later Col) <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Kittinger" title="Joseph Kittinger">Joseph Kittinger</a>, a USAF fighter pilot and stratosphere balloonist, met all the requirements but preferred to stay in his contemporary project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192&#8211;93_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192&#8211;93-169">[156]</a></sup> Other potential candidates declined because they did not believe that manned spaceflight had a future beyond Project Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192&#8211;93_169-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192&#8211;93-169">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[n 14]</a></sup> From the original 508, 110 candidates were selected for an interview, and from the interviews, 32 were selected for further physical and mental testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200193_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200193-174">[160]</a></sup>  Their health, vision, and hearing were examined, together with their tolerance to noise, vibrations, g-forces, personal isolation, and heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200198_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200198-175">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[162]</a></sup> In a special chamber, they were tested to see if they could perform their tasks under confusing conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200198_175-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200198-175">[161]</a></sup> The candidates had to answer more than 500 questions about themselves and describe what they saw in different images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200198_175-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200198-175">[161]</a></sup> Navy Lt (later Capt) <a href="/wiki/Jim_Lovell" title="Jim Lovell">Jim Lovell</a>, who was later an astronaut in the <a href="/wiki/Project_Gemini" title="Project Gemini">Gemini</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo programs</a>, did not pass the physical tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192&#8211;93_169-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200192&#8211;93-169">[156]</a></sup> After these tests it was intended to narrow the group down to six astronauts, but in the end it was decided to keep seven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200194_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200194-177">[163]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The astronauts went through a training program covering some of the same exercises that were used in their selection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001103-110_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001103-110-45">[41]</a></sup> They simulated the g-force profiles of launch and reentry in a centrifuge at the Naval Air Development Center, and were taught special breathing techniques necessary when subjected to more than 6 g.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001104_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001104-157">[145]</a></sup> Weightlessness training took place in aircraft, first on the rear seat of a two-seater fighter and later inside converted and padded <a href="/wiki/Cargo_aircraft" title="Cargo aircraft">cargo aircraft</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001105_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001105-178">[164]</a></sup> They practiced gaining control of a spinning spacecraft in a machine at the Lewis Flight Propulsion Laboratory called the Multi-Axis Spin-Test Inertia Facility (MASTIF), by using an <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude controller</a> handle simulating the one in the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[166]</a></sup> A further measure for finding the right attitude in orbit was star and Earth recognition training in planetaria and simulators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001105,_109_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001105,_109-181">[167]</a></sup> Communication and flight procedures were practiced in flight simulators, first together with a single person assisting them and later with the Mission Control Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001111_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001111-182">[168]</a></sup> Recovery was practiced in pools at Langley, and later at sea with frogmen and helicopter crews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966346_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966346-183">[169]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 789px;_width: 789px;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255px"><div style="width: 255px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 250px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Johnsville_Centrifuge.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="564" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Johnsville_Centrifuge.jpg/219px-Johnsville_Centrifuge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Johnsville_Centrifuge.jpg/328px-Johnsville_Centrifuge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Johnsville_Centrifuge.jpg/437px-Johnsville_Centrifuge.jpg 2x" width="219"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>G-force training, <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Warfare_Center_Warminster" title="Naval Air Warfare Center Warminster">Johnsville</a>, 1960\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255px"><div style="width: 255px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 250px;"><div style="margin:18px auto;"><a class="image" 2x" width="220"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Weightlessness simulation in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-131" title="C-131">C-131</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255px"><div style="width: 255px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 250px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Project_Mercury_AWT_Gimbaling_Rig_close.jpg/439px-Project_Mercury_AWT_Gimbaling_Rig_close.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>MASTIF at <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Research_Center" title="Glenn Research Center">Lewis Research Center</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left;margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 205px"><div style="width: 205px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><div //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Shepard_in_trainer_before_launch.jpg/282px-Shepard_in_trainer_before_launch.jpg 2x" width="141"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Flight trainer at Cape Canaveral\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left;margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255px"><div style="width: 255px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 250px;"><div style="margin:16px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B60_285b.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="740" data-file-width="971" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/B60_285b.jpg/220px-B60_285b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/B60_285b.jpg/330px-B60_285b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/B60_285b.jpg/440px-B60_285b.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Egress training at <a href="/wiki/Langley_Research_Center" title="Langley Research Center">Langley</a>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_profile">Mission profile</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mission profile">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suborbital">Suborbital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Suborbital">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mr3-flight-timeline-simple-2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="465" data-file-width="736" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Mr3-flight-timeline-simple-2.png/260px-Mr3-flight-timeline-simple-2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Mr3-flight-timeline-simple-2.png/390px-Mr3-flight-timeline-simple-2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Mr3-flight-timeline-simple-2.png/520px-Mr3-flight-timeline-simple-2.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mr3-flight-timeline-simple-2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Profile. See timetable for explanation. Dashed line: region of weightlessness.</div></div></div>\n<p>A Redstone rocket was used to boost the capsule for 2 minutes and 30 seconds to an altitude of 32 nautical miles (59&#160;km); the capsule continued ascending on a ballistic curve after booster separation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnknown1961a7_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnknown1961a7-184">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001208,_250_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001208,_250-185">[171]</a></sup> The launch escape system was jettisoned at the same time. At the top of the curve, the spacecraft\'s retrorockets were fired for testing purposes; they were not necessary for reentry because orbital speed had not been attained. The spacecraft landed in the Atlantic Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001250,_308_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001250,_308-186">[172]</a></sup> The suborbital mission took about 15 minutes, had an apogee altitude of 102&#8211;103 nautical miles (189&#8211;191&#160;km), and a downrange distance of 262 nautical miles (485&#160;km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640&#8211;641_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640&#8211;641-155">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001475_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001475-187">[173]</a></sup> From the time of booster-spacecraft separation until reentry where air started to slow down the spacecraft, the pilot would experience weightlessness as shown on the image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[n 15]</a></sup> The recovery procedure would be the same as an orbital mission.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2">Timetable (min:sec.)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">0:00</td>\n<td>Launch\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">2:22</td>\n<td>Launch vehicle cut-off and tower separation\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">2:32</td>\n<td>Spacecraft separation\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">2:37</td>\n<td>Turnaround\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">5:14</td>\n<td>Retrofire\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">6:14</td>\n<td>Retropack jettisoned\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">7:48</td>\n<td>Reentry\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">9:38</td>\n<td>Drogue deployed\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">10:15</td>\n<td>Main chute deployed\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:right;">15:22</td>\n<td>Landing\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital">Orbital</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Orbital">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Launch-complex-14.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="574" data-file-width="1037" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Launch-complex-14.png/260px-Launch-complex-14.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Launch-complex-14.png/390px-Launch-complex-14.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Launch-complex-14.png/520px-Launch-complex-14.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Launch-complex-14.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Launch Complex 14 just before launch (service tower rolled aside). Preparations for launch were made in the blockhouse.</div></div></div>\n<p>Preparations for a mission started a month in advance with the selection of the primary and back-up astronaut; they would practice together for the mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001110_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001110-189">[174]</a></sup> For three days prior to launch, the astronaut went through a special diet to minimize his need for <a href="/wiki/Defecation" title="Defecation">defecating</a> during the flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001278_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001278-190">[175]</a></sup> On the morning of the trip he typically ate a steak breakfast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001278_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001278-190">[175]</a></sup> After having sensors applied to his body and being dressed in the pressure suit, he started breathing pure oxygen to prepare him for the atmosphere of the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001280_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001280-191">[176]</a></sup> He arrived at the launch pad, took the elevator up the launch tower and entered the spacecraft two hours before launch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188-192">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[n 16]</a></sup> Once the astronaut was secured inside, the hatch was bolted, the launch area evacuated and the mobile tower rolled back.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001281_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001281-193">[178]</a></sup> After this, the launch vehicle was filled with liquid oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001281_193-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001281-193">[178]</a></sup> The entire procedure of preparing for launch and launching the spacecraft followed a time table called the countdown. It started a day in advance with a pre-count, in which all systems of the launch vehicle and spacecraft were checked. After that followed a 15-hour hold, during which pyrotechnics were installed. Then came the main countdown which for orbital flights started 6&#189; hours before launch (T &#8211; 390 min), counted backwards to launch (T = 0) and then forward until orbital insertion (T + 5 min).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188-192">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[n 17]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vol-Atlas-Mercury.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="968" data-file-width="819" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Vol-Atlas-Mercury.png/200px-Vol-Atlas-Mercury.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Vol-Atlas-Mercury.png/300px-Vol-Atlas-Mercury.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Vol-Atlas-Mercury.png/400px-Vol-Atlas-Mercury.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vol-Atlas-Mercury.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Profile. A-C: launch. D: insert into orbit. E-K: reentry and landing</div></div></div>\n<p>On an orbital mission, the Atlas\' rocket engines were ignited four seconds before lift-off. The launch vehicle was held to the ground by clamps and then released when sufficient thrust was built up at lift-off (<b>A</b>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188,_242_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188,_242-197">[180]</a></sup> After 30 seconds of flight, the point of maximum <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_pressure" title="Dynamic pressure">dynamic pressure</a> against the vehicle was reached, at which the astronaut felt heavy vibrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001340_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001340-198">[181]</a></sup> After 2 minutes and 10 seconds, the two outboard booster engines shut down and were released with the aft skirt, leaving the center sustainer engine running (<b>B</b>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188_192-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188-192">[177]</a></sup> At this point, the launch escape system was no longer needed, and was separated from the spacecraft by its jettison rocket (<b>C</b>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132-61">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[n 18]</a></sup> The space vehicle moved gradually to a horizontal attitude until, at an altitude of 87 nautical miles (161&#160;km), the sustainer engine shut down and the spacecraft was inserted into orbit (<b>D</b>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnknown196246_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnknown196246-201">[183]</a></sup> This happened after 5 minutes and 10 seconds in a direction pointing east, whereby the spacecraft would gain speed from the rotation of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188,_460_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001188,_460-202">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[n 19]</a></sup> Here the spacecraft fired the three posigrade rockets for a second to separate it from the launch vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001133_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001133-205">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[n 20]</a></sup> Just before orbital insertion and sustainer engine cutoff, g-loads peaked at 8 g (6 g for a suborbital flight).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001340_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001340-198">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnknown196110_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnknown196110-208">[188]</a></sup> In orbit, the spacecraft automatically turned 180&#176;, pointed the retropackage forward and its nose 14.5&#176; downward and kept this attitude for the rest of the orbital phase of the mission, as it was necessary for communication with the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966333_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966333-209">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001120_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001120-210">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[n 21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Once in orbit, it was not possible for the spacecraft to change its <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectory</a> except by initiating reentry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001462_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001462-213">[192]</a></sup> Each orbit would typically take 88 minutes to complete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001324_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001324-214">[193]</a></sup> The lowest point of the orbit, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perigee" title="Perigee">perigee</a>, was at about 87 nautical miles (161&#160;km) altitude, and the highest point, called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apogee" title="Apogee">apogee</a>, was about 150 nautical miles (280&#160;km) altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001475_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001475-187">[173]</a></sup> When leaving orbit (<b>E</b>), the angle of retrofire was 34&#176; downward from the flight path angle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966333_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966333-209">[189]</a></sup> Retrorockets fired for 10 seconds each (<b>F</b>) in a sequence where one started 5 seconds after the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001133_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001133-205">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnknown19619_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnknown19619-215">[194]</a></sup> During reentry (<b>G</b>), the astronaut would experience about 8 g (11&#8211;12 g on a suborbital mission).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966574_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966574-216">[195]</a></sup> The temperature around the heat shield rose to 3,000&#160;&#176;F (1,600&#160;&#176;C) and at the same time, there was a two-minute radio blackout due to <a href="/wiki/Ionization" title="Ionization">ionization</a> of the air around the spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnknown19629_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnknown19629-217">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001134_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001134-63">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After reentry, a small, drogue parachute (<b>H</b>) was deployed at 21,000&#160;ft (6,400&#160;m) for stabilizing the spacecraft\'s descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001147_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001147-74">[68]</a></sup> The main parachute (<b>I</b>) was deployed at 10,000&#160;ft (3,000&#160;m) starting with a narrow opening that opened fully in a few seconds to lessen the strain on the lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966356_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966356-218">[197]</a></sup> Just before hitting the water, the landing bag inflated from behind the heat shield to reduce the force of impact (<b>J</b>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966356_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966356-218">[197]</a></sup> Upon landing the parachutes were released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144-71">[65]</a></sup> An antenna (<b>K</b>) was raised and sent out signals that could be traced by ships and <a href="/wiki/Helicopter" title="Helicopter">helicopters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144-71">[65]</a></sup> Further, a green marker dye was spread around the spacecraft to make its location more visible from the air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144-71">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[n 22]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frogmen" title="Frogmen">Frogmen</a> brought in by helicopters inflated a collar around the craft to keep it upright in the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001166_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001166-221">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[n 23]</a></sup> The recovery helicopter hooked onto the spacecraft and the astronaut blew the escape hatch to exit the capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144&#8211;145_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001144&#8211;145-70">[64]</a></sup> He was then hoisted aboard the helicopter that finally brought both him and the spacecraft to the ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[n 24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215px"><div style="width: 215px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 210px;"><div style="margin:15.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_profile.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="694" data-file-width="784" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Mercury_profile.jpg/180px-Mercury_profile.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Mercury_profile.jpg/270px-Mercury_profile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Mercury_profile.jpg/360px-Mercury_profile.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Mercury manned launches\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 250px"><div style="width: 250px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 245px;"><div style="margin:15.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glenn62.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1127" data-file-width="1528" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Glenn62.jpg/215px-Glenn62.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Glenn62.jpg/323px-Glenn62.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Glenn62.jpg/430px-Glenn62.jpg 2x" width="215"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>John Glenn in orbit (Mercury-Atlas 6)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 250px"><div style="width: 250px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 245px;"><div style="margin:16px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shepard_Hoisted_into_Recovery_Helicopter_-_GPN-2000-001361-crop.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1599" data-file-width="2171" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Shepard_Hoisted_into_Recovery_Helicopter_-_GPN-2000-001361-crop.jpg/215px-Shepard_Hoisted_into_Recovery_Helicopter_-_GPN-2000-001361-crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Shepard_Hoisted_into_Recovery_Helicopter_-_GPN-2000-001361-crop.jpg/323px-Shepard_Hoisted_into_Recovery_Helicopter_-_GPN-2000-001361-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Shepard_Hoisted_into_Recovery_Helicopter_-_GPN-2000-001361-crop.jpg/430px-Shepard_Hoisted_into_Recovery_Helicopter_-_GPN-2000-001361-crop.jpg 2x" width="215"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Recovery seen from helicopter (Mercury-Redstone 3)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ground_control">Ground control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Ground control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_Control_crop.jpg"><img alt="A look inside the Mercury Control Center, Cape Canaveral, Florida. Dominated by the control board showing the position of the spacecraft above ground" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="806" data-file-width="1215" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Mercury_Control_crop.jpg/220px-Mercury_Control_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Mercury_Control_crop.jpg/330px-Mercury_Control_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Mercury_Control_crop.jpg/440px-Mercury_Control_crop.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_Control_crop.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Inside Control Center at Cape Canaveral (Mercury-Atlas 8)</div></div></div>\n<p>The number of personnel supporting a Mercury mission was typically around 18,000, with about 15,000 people associated with recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966508_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966508-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnknown19623_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnknown19623-224">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[n 25]</a></sup> Most of the others followed the spacecraft from the World Wide Tracking Network, a chain of 18 stations placed around the equator, which was based on a network used for satellites and made ready in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001124,_461&#8211;462_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001124,_461&#8211;462-227">[202]</a></sup> It collected data from the spacecraft and provided two-way communication between the astronaut and the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001117_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001117-228">[203]</a></sup> Each station had a range of 700 nautical miles (1,300&#160;km) and a pass typically lasted 7 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001121,_126_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001121,_126-229">[204]</a></sup> Mercury astronauts on the ground would take part of the Capsule Communicator or CAPCOM who communicated with the astronaut in orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966360_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966360-230">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966479_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966479-231">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[n 26]</a></sup> Data from the spacecraft was sent to the ground, processed at the Goddard Space Center and relayed to the <a href="/wiki/Mercury_Control_Center" title="Mercury Control Center">Mercury Control Center</a> at Cape Canaveral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001118_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001118-233">[207]</a></sup> In the Control Center, the data was displayed on boards on each side of a world map, which showed the position of the spacecraft, its <a href="/wiki/Ground_track" title="Ground track">ground track</a> and the place it could land in an emergency within the next 30 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001120_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001120-210">[190]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The World Wide Tracking Network went on to serve subsequent space programs, until it was replaced by a satellite relay system in the 1980s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001409_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001409-234">[208]</a></sup> Mission Control Center was moved from Cape Canaveral to Houston in 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole200188_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole200188-235">[209]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="box-sizing:border-box;width:100%;font-size:95%;padding:4px;border:none;border:solid 1px #aaa;background:#F9F9F9;padding-left:10px;"><div style="font-size:100%;line-height:1.6;font-weight:bold;background:transparent;text-align:left;;"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Tracking network</div></div><div class="mw-collapsible-content" style="background:transparent;text-align:left;;">\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 795px"><div style="width: 795px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 790px;"><div style="margin:17px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_Tracking_Network_2.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="598" data-file-width="1018" decoding="async" height="446" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Mercury_Tracking_Network_2.png/760px-Mercury_Tracking_Network_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3d/Mercury_Tracking_Network_2.png 1.5x" width="760"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Ground track and tracking stations for Mercury-Atlas 8. Spacecraft starts from Cape Canaveral in Florida and moves east; each new orbit-track is displaced to the left due to the rotation of the Earth. It moves between latitudes 32.5&#176; north and 32.5&#176; south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001128_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001128-236">[210]</a></sup> Key: 1&#8211;6: orbit number. Yellow: launch. Black dot: tracking station. Red: range of station; Blue: landing.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n</div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flights">Flights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Flights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div style="float: right; position: relative;width:400px; margin: 0em 0em 0.5em 2em;">\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury-splash-down.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="273" data-file-width="702" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Mercury-splash-down.png/400px-Mercury-splash-down.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Mercury-splash-down.png/600px-Mercury-splash-down.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b0/Mercury-splash-down.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury-splash-down.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Project Mercury landing sites</div></div></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:274px; top:64px"><span style="color:gray;">/</span></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:270px; top:52px"><span style="background:white; color:gray; font-size:11px; padding-left:2px;">Cape Canaveral</span></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:34px; top:102px"><span style="color:gray;font-size:11px;">Hawaii</span></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:284px; top:68px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:City_locator_23.svg"><img alt="City locator 23.svg" data-file-height="16" data-file-width="16" decoding="async" height="10" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/City_locator_23.svg/10px-City_locator_23.svg.png" style="font-size:11px;">Friendship 7</span></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:324px; top:92px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:City_locator_23.svg"><img alt="City locator 23.svg" data-file-height="16" data-file-width="16" decoding="async" height="10" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/City_locator_23.svg/10px-City_locator_23.svg.png" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/City_locator_23.svg/20px-City_locator_23.svg.png 2x" width="10"/></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:334px; top:94px"><span style="font-size:11px;">Aurora 7</span></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:4px; top:70px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:City_locator_23.svg"><img alt="City locator 23.svg" data-file-height="16" data-file-width="16" decoding="async" height="10" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/City_locator_23.svg/10px-City_locator_23.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/City_locator_23.svg/15px-City_locator_23.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/City_locator_23.svg/20px-City_locator_23.svg.png 2x" width="10"/></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:14px; top:70px"><span style="font-size:11px;">Sigma 7</span></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:2px; top:82px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:City_locator_23.svg"><img alt="City locator 23.svg" data-file-height="16" data-file-width="16" decoding="async" height="10" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/City_locator_23.svg/10px-City_locator_23.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/City_locator_23.svg/15px-City_locator_23.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/City_locator_23.svg/20px-City_locator_23.svg.png 2x" width="10"/></a></div>\n<div style="position:absolute; left:12px; top:84px"><span style="font-size:11px;">Faith 7</span></div>\n</div>\n<p>On April 12, 1961 the Soviet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cosmonaut" title="Cosmonaut">cosmonaut</a> <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> became the first person in space on an orbital flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966332_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966332-237">[211]</a></sup> Alan Shepard became the first American in space on a suborbital flight three weeks later, on May 5, 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966341_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966341-150">[138]</a></sup> John Glenn, the third Mercury astronaut to fly, became the first American to reach orbit on February 20, 1962, but only after the Soviets had launched a second cosmonaut, Gherman Titov, into a day-long flight in August 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966377,_422_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966377,_422-238">[212]</a></sup> Three more Mercury orbital flights were made, ending on May 16, 1963 with a day-long, 22 orbit flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640&#8211;641_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640&#8211;641-155">[143]</a></sup> However, the Soviet Union ended its <a href="/wiki/Vostok_programme" title="Vostok programme">Vostok program</a> the next month, with the human spaceflight endurance record set by the 82-orbit, almost 5-day <a href="/wiki/Vostok_5" title="Vostok 5">Vostok 5</a> flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001476_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001476-239">[213]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manned">Manned</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Manned">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>All of the six manned Mercury flights were successful, though some planned flights were canceled during the project (see below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640-641_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640-641-240">[214]</a></sup> The main medical problems encountered were simple personal <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a>, and post-flight <a href="/wiki/Orthostatic_hypotension" title="Orthostatic hypotension">symptoms of low blood pressure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966508_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966508-3">[2]</a></sup> The launch vehicles had been tested through unmanned flights, therefore the numbering of manned missions did not start with 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966638-641_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966638-641-241">[215]</a></sup> Also, there were two separately numbered series: MR for "Mercury-Redstone" (suborbital flights), and MA for "Mercury-Atlas" (orbital flights). These names were not popularly used, since the astronauts followed a pilot tradition, each giving their spacecraft a name. They selected names ending with a "7" to commemorate the seven astronauts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132-61">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640-149">[137]</a></sup> Times given are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">Universal Coordinated Time</a>, local time + 5 hours. MA = Mercury-Atlas, MR = Mercury-Redstone, LC = Launch Complex. Source:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[n 27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th rowspan="2">Mission\n</th>\n<th February 1962.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 295px"><div style="width: 295px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 290px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_Kearsarge_(CVS-33)_crew_spells_out_%27Mercury_9%27_on_the_flight_deck,_15_May_1963_(GPN-2000-001403).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1953" data-file-width="2924" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/USS_Kearsarge_%28CVS-33%29_crew_spells_out_%27Mercury_9%27_on_the_flight_deck%2C_15_May_1963_%28GPN-2000-001403%29.jpg/255px-USS_Kearsarge_%28CVS-33%29_crew_spells_out_%27Mercury_9%27_on_the_flight_deck%2C_15_May_1963_%28GPN-2000-001403%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/USS_Kearsarge_%28CVS-33%29_crew_spells_out_%27Mercury_9%27_on_the_flight_deck%2C_15_May_1963_%28GPN-2000-001403%29.jpg/382px-USS_Kearsarge_%28CVS-33%29_crew_spells_out_%27Mercury_9%27_on_the_flight_deck%2C_15_May_1963_%28GPN-2000-001403%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/USS_Kearsarge_%28CVS-33%29_crew_spells_out_%27Mercury_9%27_on_the_flight_deck%2C_15_May_1963_%28GPN-2000-001403%29.jpg/509px-USS_Kearsarge_%28CVS-33%29_crew_spells_out_%27Mercury_9%27_on_the_flight_deck%2C_15_May_1963_%28GPN-2000-001403%29.jpg 2x" width="255"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>USS <i>Kearsarge</i> with crew spelling Mercury-9. May 1963.\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unmanned">Unmanned</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Unmanned">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The 20 unmanned flights used Little Joe, Redstone, and Atlas launch vehicles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640-149">[137]</a></sup> They were used to develop the launch vehicles, launch escape system, spacecraft and tracking network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966638-641_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966638-641-241">[215]</a></sup> One flight of a <a href="/wiki/Scout_(rocket_family)" title="Scout (rocket family)">Scout rocket</a> attempted to launch an unmanned satellite for testing the ground tracking network, but failed to reach orbit.  The Little Joe program used seven airframes for eight flights, of which three were successful. The second Little Joe flight was named Little Joe 6, because it was inserted into the program after the first 5 airframes had been allocated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001231_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001231-264">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001278_190-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001278-190">[175]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Mission<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[n 1\n</td>\n<td>Due to an electrical malfunction, the escape tower ignited &#189; hour before launch and took the spacecraft with it, leaving the rocket on the ground.<sup class="reference" Mercury-Atlas flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640_149-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966640-149">[137]</a></sup> Recovered by <a ablative heatshield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001229_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001229-114">[105]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Little Joe 6\n</td>\n<td>No additional tests<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966209_272-0"><a id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966210_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966210-273">[239]</a></sup> Recovered by <a href="/wiki/USS_Opportune_(ARS-41)" title="USS Opportune (ARS-41)">USS&#160;<i>Opportune</i></a> 11.5&#160;mi (18.5&#160;km) SE of Wallops Island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001232_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001232-274">[240]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Little Joe 2\n</td>\n<td>Carried Sam, a <a href="/wiki/Rhesus_macaque" title="Rhesus macaque">rhesus macaque</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966210_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966210-273">[239]</a></sup> Recovered by <a href="/wiki/USS_Borie_(DD-704)" title="USS Borie (DD-704)">USS&#160;<i>Borie</i></a> 194&#160;mi (312&#160;km) SE of Wallops Island, Virginia; altitude: 53&#160;mi (85&#160;km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001234,_474_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001234,_474-275">[241]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Little Joe 1B\n</td>\n<td>Carried a female rhesus monkey named Miss Sam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966212_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966212-276">[242]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Beach Abort\n</td>\n<td>A boilerplate spacecraft was lifted from the ground by the launch escape system alone at <a href="/wiki/Wallops_Island" title="Wallops Island">Wallops Island</a>. It reached an apogee of 0.751 kilometres (2,465&#160;ft) and was recovered after landing. Top velocity: 436 metres per second (976&#160;mph). Total payload: 1,154&#160;kg.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Mercury-Atlas 1\n</td>\n<td>Exploded while passing through max-q<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966276_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966276-277">[243]</a></sup> To save weight, the airframe had been made thinner since Big Joe, which led to a collapse. The next Atlas was strengthened by a temporary solution while the rest were made from the same specifications as Big Joe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001243_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001243-278">[244]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Little Joe 5\n</td>\n<td>The clamp holding the spacecraft was deflected by air pressure; due to this and an incorrect wiring, the escape tower ignited too early and further failed to separate spacecraft from launch vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001248_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001248-47">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[n 35]</a></sup> Altitude: 10&#160;mi (16&#160;km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966291_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966291-280">[245]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Mercury-Redstone 1\n</td>\n<td>Engine shutdown caused by improper separation of electrical cables;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966298_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966298-281">[246]</a></sup> vehicle rose 4&#160;in (10&#160;cm) and settled back on the pad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966294_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966294-282">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[n 36]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Mercury-Redstone 1A\n</td>\n<td>First flight of Mercury / Redstone. Recovered by <a href="/wiki/USS_Valley_Forge_(CV-45)" title="USS Valley Forge (CV-45)">USS&#160;<i>Valley Forge</i></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966297_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966297-284">[248]</a></sup> Altitude: 130&#160;mi (210&#160;km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966310_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966310-119">[110]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Mercury-Redstone 2\n</td>\n<td>Carried the chimpanzee Ham on suborbital flight. Recovered by <a href="/wiki/USS_Donner_(LSD-20)" title="USS Donner (LSD-20)">USS&#160;<i>Donner</i></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966316_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966316-285">[249]</a></sup> 422&#160;mi (679&#160;km) SE of Cape Canaveral; altitude: 157&#160;mi (253&#160;km)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966638-639_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966638-639-286">[250]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Mercury-Atlas 2\n</td>\n<td>Recovered by USS <i>Donner</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966321&#8211;322_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966321&#8211;322-287">[251]</a></sup> 1,432&#160;mi (2,305&#160;km) SE of Cape Canaveral.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Little Joe 5A\n</td>\n<td>Tower fired 14 seconds too soon; it failed to separate the spacecraft from the rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966327_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966327-288">[252]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td nowrap="">Mercury-Redstone BD\n</td>\n<td>BD: Booster Development)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966330_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966330-289">[253]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Mercury-Atlas 3\n</td>\n<td>Upgraded from suborbital flight. Was aborted when it did not go into orbit; boiler plate capsule recovered and reused in Mercury-Atlas 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966337_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966337-290">[254]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Little Joe 5B\n</td>\n<td>Concluded Little Joe program.\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="background-color:#CDE;">\n<td>Mercury-Atlas 4\n</td>\n<td>Completed one orbit and sent data to the ground; first orbital flight of the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966386-387_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966386-387-291">[255]</a></sup> Recovery by <a href="/wiki/USS_Decatur_(DD-936)" title="USS Decatur (DD-936)">USS&#160;<i>Decatur</i></a> 176&#160;mi (283&#160;km) east of <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966389_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966389-292">[256]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="background-color:#CDE;">\n<td>Mercury-Scout 1\n</td>\n<td>Was aborted after malfunction of guidance system;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966397_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966397-293">[257]</a></sup> results of Mercury-Atlas 4 and Mercury-Atlas 5 were used instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001312_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001312-294">[258]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="background-color:#CDE;">\n<td>Mercury-Atlas 5\n</td>\n<td>Chimpanzee Enos completed a two-orbit flight, performing tasks to prove it possible for a person to function during a flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966404_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966404-295">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[n 37]</a></sup> Last Mercury-Atlas test flight. Recovery by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USS_Stormes_(DD-780)" title="USS Stormes (DD-780)">USS&#160;<i>Stormes</i></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966406_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966406-298">[261]</a></sup> 255&#160;mi (410&#160;km) SE of Bermuda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963169_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963169-299">[262]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 255px"><div style="width: 255px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 250px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" 2x" width="218"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Mercury-Atlas 5: Enos, 1961\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canceled">Canceled</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Canceled">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Nine of the planned flights were canceled. Suborbital flights were planned for four other astronauts but the number of flights was cut down gradually and finally all remaining were canceled after Titov\'s flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966377_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966377-300">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001474_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001474-301">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[n 38]</a></sup> Mercury-Atlas 9 was intended to be followed by more one-day flights and even a three-day flight but with the coming of the Gemini Project it seemed unnecessary. The Jupiter booster was, as mentioned above, intended to be used for different purposes.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Mission\n</th>\n<th>Pilot\n</th>\n<th>Planned
