11\n</td>\n<td>Grissom\n</td>\n<td>Fourth Quarter, 1963\n</td>\n<td>October 1962<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mercury-Atlas_11_311-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mercury-Atlas_11-311">[271]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Mercury-Atlas 12\n</td>\n<td>Schirra\n</td>\n<td>Fourth Quarter, 1963\n</td>\n<td>October 1962<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mercury-Atlas_12_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mercury-Atlas_12-312">[272]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gordon_cooper_ticker_tape.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="959" data-file-width="869" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Gordon_cooper_ticker_tape.png/190px-Gordon_cooper_ticker_tape.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Gordon_cooper_ticker_tape.png/285px-Gordon_cooper_ticker_tape.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Gordon_cooper_ticker_tape.png/380px-Gordon_cooper_ticker_tape.png 2x" width="190"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gordon_cooper_ticker_tape.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ticker_tape_parade" title="Ticker tape parade">Ticker tape parade</a> for Gordon Cooper, 1963</div></div></div>\n<p>Today the Mercury program is commemorated as the first manned American space program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001cover_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001cover-313">[273]</a></sup> It did not win the race against the Soviet Union, but gave back national prestige and was scientifically a successful precursor of later programs such as Gemini, Apollo and Skylab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001417_314-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001417-314">[274]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[n 41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1950s, some experts doubted that manned spaceflight was possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[n 42]</a></sup> Still when <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> was elected president, many including he had doubts about the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966272_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966272-319">[277]</a></sup> As president he chose to support the programs a few months before the launch of <i>Freedom 7</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966306_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966306-320">[278]</a></sup> which became a great public success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966434_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966434-321">[279]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[n 43]</a></sup> Afterwards, a majority of the American public supported manned spaceflight, and within a few weeks, Kennedy announced a plan for a manned mission to land on the Moon and return safely to Earth before the end of the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966363_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966363-326">[283]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The six astronauts who flew were awarded medals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966362,_435,_459,_486,_502,_584_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966362,_435,_459,_486,_502,_584-327">[284]</a></sup> driven in parades and two of them were invited to address a <a href="/wiki/Joint_session_of_the_United_States_Congress" title="Joint session of the United States Congress">joint session of the US Congress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966435,_501_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966435,_501-328">[285]</a></sup> As a response to the selection criteria, which ruled out women, a private project was founded in which 13 women pilots successfully underwent the same tests as the men in Project Mercury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001447_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001447-329">[286]</a></sup> It was named <a href="/wiki/Mercury_13" title="Mercury 13">Mercury 13</a> by the media<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001447&#8211;448_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001447&#8211;448-330">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[n 44]</a></sup> Despite this effort, NASA did not select female astronauts <a href="/wiki/NASA_Astronaut_Group_8" title="NASA Astronaut Group 8">until 1978</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001448_332-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001448-332">[288]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1964, a monument commemorating Project Mercury was unveiled near Launch Complex 14 at Cape Canaveral, featuring a metal logo combining the symbol of Mercury with the number 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Monument_333-0"><a href="#cite_note-Monument-333">[289]</a></sup>  In 1962, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> honored the Mercury-Atlas 6 flight with a Project Mercury commemorative stamp, the first US postal issue to depict a manned spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlennStamp_334-0"><a href="#cite_note-GlennStamp-334">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[n 45]</a></sup> On film, the program was portrayed in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Right_Stuff_(film)" title="The Right Stuff (film)">The Right Stuff</a></i> a 1983 adaptation of <a href="/wiki/Tom_Wolfe" title="Tom Wolfe">Tom Wolfe</a>\'s 1979 <a href="/wiki/The_Right_Stuff_(book)" title="The Right Stuff (book)">book of the same name</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMdBRightStuff_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMdBRightStuff-337">[292]</a></sup> On February 25, 2011, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Institute_of_Electrical_and_Electronic_Engineers" title="Institute of Electrical and Electronic Engineers">Institute of Electrical and Electronic Engineers</a>, the world\'s largest technical professional society, awarded <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> (the successor company to McDonnell Aircraft) a Milestone Award for important inventions which debuted on the Mercury spacecraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoeingMedia_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-BoeingMedia-338">[293]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[n 46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 335px"><div style="width: 335px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 330px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Project_Mercury_Pad14.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1241" data-file-width="1862" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Project_Mercury_Pad14.jpg/300px-Project_Mercury_Pad14.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Project_Mercury_Pad14.jpg/450px-Project_Mercury_Pad14.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Project_Mercury_Pad14.jpg/600px-Project_Mercury_Pad14.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Mercury monument at Launch Complex 14, 1964\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="float:left; margin:0em 0.15em 0em 0em;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 405px"><div style="width: 405px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 400px;"><div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Project_Mercury_4%C2%A2_US_Postage_stamp_February_20,_1962_FDC_Scott_-1193.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1029" data-file-width="1881" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Project_Mercury_4%C2%A2_US_Postage_stamp_February_20%2C_1962_FDC_Scott_-1193.jpg/366px-Project_Mercury_4%C2%A2_US_Postage_stamp_February_20%2C_1962_FDC_Scott_-1193.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Project_Mercury_4%C2%A2_US_Postage_stamp_February_20%2C_1962_FDC_Scott_-1193.jpg/549px-Project_Mercury_4%C2%A2_US_Postage_stamp_February_20%2C_1962_FDC_Scott_-1193.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Project_Mercury_4%C2%A2_US_Postage_stamp_February_20%2C_1962_FDC_Scott_-1193.jpg/732px-Project_Mercury_4%C2%A2_US_Postage_stamp_February_20%2C_1962_FDC_Scott_-1193.jpg 2x" width="366"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Commemorative <a href="/wiki/U.S._space_exploration_history_on_U.S._stamps#Project_Mercury_Issue_of_1962" title="U.S. space exploration history on U.S. stamps">Project Mercury 4&#162; US Postage stamp</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[n 47]</a></sup>\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Displays">Displays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Displays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The spacecraft that flew, together with some that did not, are on display in the United States. <i>Friendship 7</i> (capsule No. 13) went on a global tour, popularly known as its "fourth orbit". <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966436_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966436-341">[294]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 744px;_width: 744px;">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 240px"><div style="width: 240px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 235px;"><div style="margin:16px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Freedom_7_U.S._Naval_Academy.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1270" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Freedom_7_U.S._Naval_Academy.JPG/205px-Freedom_7_U.S._Naval_Academy.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Freedom_7_U.S._Naval_Academy.JPG/308px-Freedom_7_U.S._Naval_Academy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Freedom_7_U.S._Naval_Academy.JPG/410px-Freedom_7_U.S._Naval_Academy.JPG 2x" width="205"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><i>Freedom class="mw-headline" id="Space_program_comparison">Space program comparison</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Space program comparison">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 465px"><div style="width: 465px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 460px;"><div style="margin:16px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NASA_spacecraft_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3559" data-file-width="4669" decoding="async" height="328" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/NASA_spacecraft_comparison.jpg/430px-NASA_spacecraft_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/NASA_spacecraft_comparison.jpg/645px-NASA_spacecraft_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/NASA_spacecraft_comparison.jpg/860px-NASA_spacecraft_comparison.jpg 2x" width="430"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>NASA illustration comparing boosters and spacecraft from Apollo (biggest), Gemini and Mercury (smallest).\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Designed in 1964 from Mercury Seven astronaut memorial</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The project was delayed by 22 months, counting from the beginning until the first orbital mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966508_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966508-3">[2]</a></sup> It had a dozen prime contractors, 75 major subcontractors, and about 7200 third-tier subcontractors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966508_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966508-3">[2]</a></sup> The cost estimate made by NASA in 1969 was $392.6 million, broken down as follows: Spacecraft: $135.3 million, launch vehicles: $82.9 million, operations: $49.3 million, tracking operations and equipment: $71.9 million and facilities: $53.2 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilford196967_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilford196967-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966643_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966643-5">[4]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Man in Space Soonest was the first part of a four-phase Moon landing program estimated to finish in 1965, cost a total of $1.5 billion ($12.9&#160;billion adjusted for inflation), and be launched by a "Super Titan" rocket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196691_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196691-11">[9]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The name <i>Little Joe</i> was adopted by its designers from the throw of a double deuce in a <a href="/wiki/Craps" title="Craps">craps</a> game since this resembled the four-rocket arrangement in the blueprints for the vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966124_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966124-37">[34]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">NASA\'s planning for recovery operations in the summer of 1960 was, according to the Navy, asking for the deployment of the whole Atlantic Fleet and might have cost more than the entire Mercury program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966265_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966265-49">[45]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On the first suborbital flight there was no urine collection whereas on the other, the astronaut had a reservoir added to the space suit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966368_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966368-68">[63]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The decision to eliminate the use of any gas but oxygen was crystalized when a serious accident occurred on April 21, 1960, in which McDonnell Aircraft test pilot G.B. North passed out and was seriously injured when testing a Mercury cabin/spacesuit atmosphere system in a vacuum chamber. The problem was found to be nitrogen-rich (oxygen-poor) air leaking from the cabin into his spacesuit feed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGiblin1998_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGiblin1998-86">[80]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Moisture and urine was reclycled into drinking water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196647_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.196647-54">[49]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-113">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The rocket plane approach to manned space flight was pursued by the Air Force with their <a href="/wiki/Boeing_X-20_Dyna-Soar" title="Boeing X-20 Dyna-Soar">Dyna-Soar</a> project, which was canceled in 1963<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001425,_428_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001425,_428-110">[102]</a></sup> Toward the end of the 1960s, NASA began the development of a reusable space plane, which was ultimately developed into the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup> The first rocket plane to enter space was an X-15 in 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[104]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Test and rework of Mercury-Redstone 2 at the Hangar required 110 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966310_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966310-119">[110]</a></sup> Hangar S was also the place where the chimpanzees were trained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966312_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966312-120">[111]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">They received a letter designation after their number, examples 2B, 15B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963216-218_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963216-218-124">[114]</a></sup> Some were modified twice; for example, spacecraft 15 became 15A and then 15B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963149_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963149-125">[115]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the time word booster was sometimes used for the first stage of the launch stack. Later the word booster came to refer to additional single-stage rockets attached to the sides of the main launch vehicle, as in Space Shuttle boosters.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-166"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-166">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Armstrong left the Navy as a <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_(junior_grade)" title="Lieutenant (junior grade)">Lieutenant, Junior Grade</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Reserve" title="United States Navy Reserve">US Naval Reserve</a>, until resigning his commission 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHansen2005118_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHansen2005118-165">[153]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-173"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-173">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the beginning of the project both President Eisenhower and NASA\'s first administrator T. K. Glennan believed that US would put the first man in space, and that this would be the end of the Space Race.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001407_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001407-172">[159]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-188"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-188">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">With the exception of the 20 seconds of retrofire during which the pilot would experience g-force.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-194"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-194">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Inside the spacecraft the other astronauts had usually prepared a practical joke, such as a sign saying "No handball playing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001281_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001281-193">[178]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-196"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-196">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Countdown was controlled from the blockhouse at the Launch Complex until 2 min. before launch then it was transferred to Mission Control Center. The countdown of the last 10 sec. before launch would be given to the astronaut by one of the others and included on TV transmissions which had already started.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001282_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001282-195">[179]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the event of a launch abort before this point, the launch escape system would fire its main rocket for one second, pulling the spacecraft and astronaut away from the launch vehicle and a possible explosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001179_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001179-77">[71]</a></sup> At this point, the spacecraft could be separated from the launch vehicle and land using its parachute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001180_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001180-199">[182]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The direction of insertion was east and slightly to the north, this meant that on at three orbit flight the tracking network was used best possible and a landing could take place in the North Atlantic Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966215_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966215-203">[185]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-207">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The sustainer would disintegrate and fall down; after the launch of <i>Friendship 7</i> a part of the sustainer was found in South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963164_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimwood1963164-206">[187]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-212"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-212">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The capsule\'s tendency to drift was countered automatically by the attitude control system (ASCS) which used small hydrogen peroxide thrusters. To save fuel, however, the spacecraft would be allowed to drift from time to time, especially on longer missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966195,_450_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966195,_450-211">[191]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-220"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-220">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radar_chaff" title="Radar chaff">Radar chaff</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SOFAR_bomb" title="SOFAR bomb">SOFAR bomb</a> which could be detected by the recovery ship\'s <a href="/wiki/Hydrophone" title="Hydrophone">hydrophone</a> were eliminated as unnecessary measures after the first orbital flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966445_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966445-219">[198]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-222">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The collar was not ready for suborbital missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001166_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001166-221">[199]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-223"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-223">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was also possible to exit the capsule through the nose cylinder; only Carpenter did this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966143_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966143-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001147_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001147-74">[68]</a></sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-226"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-226">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> <a href="/wiki/T._J._O%27Malley" title="T. J. O\'Malley">T. J. O\'Malley</a> pushed the button to launch Glenn while site Manager and Launch Conductor at Complex 14, Calvin D. Fowler, pushed the button to launch Carpenter, Schirra and Cooper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[201]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (June 2013)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-232"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-232">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Occasionally this communication was broadcast on live TV while the spacecraft was passing over the United States.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-242"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-242">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Alexander &amp; al., 1966, pp. 638&#8211;641. </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-244"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-244">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It was recovered in 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001402&#8211;405_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001402&#8211;405-123">[113]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-249"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-249">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Launch of <i>Friendship 7</i> was postponed repeatedly during two months; a frustrated politician compared the spacecraft-Atlas combination to "a <a href="/wiki/Rube_Goldberg_machine" title="Rube Goldberg machine">Rube Goldberg device</a> on top of a plumber\'s nightmare".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966409,_411_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966409,_411-248">[220]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-253"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-253">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Carpenter\'s overshoot of the landing site was caused by a malfunction in the automatic stabilization, which meant that retrofire was out of line with the movement of the spacecraft<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966453-454_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966453-454-252">[223]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-256"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-256">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">During Carpenter\'s mission a seaplane from the US Air Force got to the landing site about 1&#189; hour ahead of the Navy ships and offered to pick him up.  This, however, was declined by the admiral in charge of Mercury recovery operations, which led to a Senate hearing about the incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966457_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966457-255">[225]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-262"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-262">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Likely to be so according to Alexander &amp; al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966506_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966506-261">[230]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-265"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-265">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> Source: Alexander &amp; al., 1966, pp. 638&#8211;641 when nothing else is mentioned. </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-269"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-269">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A machine that produced the same heat, vapor and CO<sub>2</sub> as an astronaut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001309_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001309-268">[235]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-279"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-279">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"> The clamp was subsequently tested by a rocket sled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001248_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001248-47">[43]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-283"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-283">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Immediately after the Redstone\'s engine shut down, the capsule\'s <a href="/wiki/Launch_escape_system" title="Launch escape system">escape rocket</a> jettisoned itself, leaving the capsule attached to the booster. The escape rocket rose to an altitude of 4,000&#160;ft (1,200&#160;m) and landed about 400&#160;yd (370&#160;m) away. Three seconds after the escape rocket fired, the capsule deployed its <a href="/wiki/Drogue_parachute" title="Drogue parachute">drogue parachute</a>; it then deployed the main and reserve parachutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966294_282-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966294-282">[247]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-297"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-297">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Was given a reward in the form a banana pellet or a punishment in the form of mild electrical shocks depending on whether or not he gave the right response to a given signal; by mistake he was sometimes given shocks on right answers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966405_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966405-296">[260]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-303"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-303">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Within the Mercury Project organization the suborbital flights were from the start criticized as being of little value and even compared to a circus act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966100_302-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966100-302">[265]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-306"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-306">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Proposed maximum dynamic pressure test for capsule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MJ2_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-MJ2-305">[267]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-310"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-310">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mercury-Atlas 10 was intended to be a three-day mission in November 1962 with extra supplies attached to the heat shield. Call-sign <i>Freedom 7-II</i>. By January 1963, it was changed to a one-day back up mission for Mercury-Atlas 9. It was canceled after the success of the latter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001385-386_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001385-386-309">[270]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-316"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-316">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">International rules required that a pilot must land safely with the spacecraft; in reality, Gagarin landed separately by parachute; however, the Soviet Union did not admit this until 1971 when their claim was no longer in danger of being challenged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiddiqi2000283_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiddiqi2000283-315">[275]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-318"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-318">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In May 1957, five months before Sputnik I, the president of McDonnell, the later prime contractor, predicted that manned spaceflight would not take place before 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966119_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966119-317">[276]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-325"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-325">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Along the roads in the US, drivers stopped to follow Freedom 7 on the radio. Later, 100 millions saw or listened to <i>Friendship 7</i>, the first orbital flight, on TV or radio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966423_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966423-322">[280]</a></sup> The launch of <i>Sigma 7</i> and <i>Faith 7</i> were relayed live via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Communication_satellite" title="Communication satellite">communication satellite</a> to television audiences in Western Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Telstar_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-Telstar-323">[281]</a></sup>  Two of the three major US networks covered Sigma 7 minute-by-minute, the rest were showing the opening of the <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966472_324-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966472-324">[282]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-331"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-331">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It gave Soviet leader Khrushchev the idea of launching the first women cosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Valentina_Tereshkova" title="Valentina Tereshkova">Valentina Tereshkova</a> on 16 June 1963.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966506_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlexander_&amp;_al.1966506-261">[230]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-336"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-336">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The stamp first went on sale in Cape Canaveral, Florida on February 20, 1962, the same day as the first manned orbital flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GlennStamp_334-1"><a href="#cite_note-GlennStamp-334">[290]</a></sup> On May 4, 2011, the Postal Service released a stamp commemorating the 50th anniversary of <i>Freedom 7</i>, the first manned flight of the project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShepardStamp_335-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShepardStamp-335">[291]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-339"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-339">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Boeing received the award in recognition of Project Mercury\'s pioneering "navigation and control instruments, autopilot, rate stabilization and control, and <a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a> systems."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BoeingMedia_338-1"><a href="#cite_note-BoeingMedia-338">[293]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-340"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-340">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The stamp was issued February 20, 1962, the day of John Glenn\'s flight in <i>Friendship 7</i>. This one has a <a href="/wiki/First_day_of_issue" title="First day of issue">First Day of Issue</a> postmark from Cape Canaveral post office.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-343"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-343">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The only patches the Mercury astronauts wore, were the NASA logo and a name tag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patches_342-1"><a href="#cite_note-patches-342">[295]</a></sup> Each manned Mercury spacecraft was painted black and decorated with a flight insignia, its call-sign, an American flag and the words United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECatchpole2001132-61">[56]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Project_Mercury&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-lafleur20100308-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-lafleur20100308_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-lafleur20100308_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news">Lafleur, Claude (2010-03-08). <a class="external text" href="http://www.thespacereview.com/article/1579/1" rel="nofollow">"Costs of US piloted programs"</a>. <i>The Space Review</i><span class="reference-accessdate">.
